        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        210 BC – second expedition to Sardinia
       </li>
       <li>
        210 BC – naval expedition to Tarentum
       </li>
       <li>
        210 BC – Roman raids on Africa
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span id="Eastern_Mediterranean_and_Ionian_Sea_.28212.E2.80.93207_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Mediterranean_and_Ionian_Sea_(212–207_BC)">
        Eastern Mediterranean and Ionian Sea (212–207 BC)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Eastern Mediterranean and Ionian Sea (212–207 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">
        First Macedonian War
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 211 BC, Rome countered the Macedonian threat with a Greek alliance of the
       <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">
        Aetolians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elis" title="Elis">
        Elis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">
        Sparta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Messenia" title="Messenia">
        Messenia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">
        Attalus I
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">
        Pergamon
       </a>
       , as well as two Roman clients, the
       <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">
        Illyrians
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleuratus" title="Pleuratus">
        Pleuratus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scerdilaidas" title="Scerdilaidas">
        Scerdilaidas
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        209 BC – Illyrian attack on Macedonia
       </li>
       <li>
        209 BC – Carthaginian naval expedition to Corcyra
       </li>
       <li>
        209 BC –
        <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Lamia" title="First Battle of Lamia">
         First Battle of Lamia
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        209 BC –
        <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Lamia" title="Second Battle of Lamia">
         Second Battle of Lamia
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        208 BC – Roman and Pergamese attack on Lemnos
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span id="Roman_victory_.28206.E2.80.93202_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_victory_(206–202_BC)">
        Roman victory (206–202 BC)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Roman victory (206–202 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span id="Western_Mediterranean_.28206.E2.80.93202_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Mediterranean_(206–202_BC)">
        Western Mediterranean (206–202 BC)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Western Mediterranean (206–202 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="Carthage.27s_last_stand_in_Iberia">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Carthage's_last_stand_in_Iberia">
        Carthage's last stand in Iberia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Carthage's last stand in Iberia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5021" data-file-width="3038" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG/170px-Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG/255px-Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG/340px-Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Escipi%C3%B3n_africano.JPG" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bust of
         <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">
          Scipio Africanus
         </a>
         from the
         <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Papyri" title="Villa of the Papyri">
          Villa of the Papyri
         </a>
         at
         <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">
          Herculaneum
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ilipa" title="Battle of Ilipa">
        Battle of Ilipa
       </a>
       , large numbers of Celtiberian mercenaries in Carthaginian service confronted a mixed army of Romans and Iberians. Scipio Africanus Major employed a clever ruse. Every day for several days, he drew up his army for battle with the Romans stationed in the centre of the line and the Iberians on the wings. But when the enemy offered battle, he would eventually decline it. By this stratagem, he convinced the Carthaginian commanders
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mago_(Barcid)" title="Mago (Barcid)">
        Mago
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Gisco" title="Hasdrubal Gisco">
        Hasdrubal Gisco
       </a>
       that they could expect the Romans to hold the centre of their line. On the day of the battle, the Roman force deployed earlier in the day and with the Romans posted on the wings of the line. In the rush to respond, the Carthaginians placed their best forces in the centre as usual, failing to spot the unusual Roman deployment. Thus the inferior Carthaginian mercenaries on the wings were severely beaten by the Romans. The Celtiberians deserted the Carthaginian camp that night. This catastrophic defeat sealed the fate of the Carthaginian presence in Iberia. It was followed by the Roman capture of
       <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">
        Gades
       </a>
       in 206 BC after the city had already rebelled against Carthaginian rule. The Tribal leaders
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indibilis" title="Indibilis">
        Indibilis
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandonius" title="Mandonius">
        Mandonius
       </a>
       (of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ausetani" title="Ausetani">
        Ausetani
       </a>
       ) thought that, after the expulsion of the Carthaginians, the Romans would leave and they would gain control of Spain again. This didn't happen, however, so they participated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_at_Sucro" title="Mutiny at Sucro">
        mutineers at the Sucro camp
       </a>
       against the Romans. This mutiny was ultimately squelched by Scipio Africanus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livy2_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Livy2-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.gutenberg.org/files/12582/12582-h/12582-h.htm#b24" rel="nofollow">
          28.24
         </a>
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 205 BC, a last attempt was made by Mago to recapture
       <a href="/wiki/Cartagena,_Spain" title="Cartagena, Spain">
        New Carthage
       </a>
       when the Roman occupiers were shaken by a mutiny and an Iberian uprising against their new overlords; the attack was repulsed. So, in the same year, Mago left Iberia, setting sail from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balearic_islands" title="Balearic islands">
        Balearic islands
       </a>
       to Italy with his remaining forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011362_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011362-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Numidian_struggle">
        The Numidian struggle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The Numidian struggle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="500" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg/200px-Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg/300px-Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg/400px-Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Massinissa_01.jpg" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tomb of the Numidian king
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massinissa" title="Massinissa">
          Massinissa
         </a>
         (c. 238–c. 148 BC). Massinissa, leader of the Massyli tribe, was originally an ally of Carthage and fought against the Romans in Iberia. But, after the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ilipa" title="Battle of Ilipa">
          Battle of Ilipa
         </a>
         in 206 BC, he switched sides. His support at the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">
          Battle of Zama
         </a>
         was critical to the Roman victory. Massinissa remained a staunch Roman ally for the rest of his long life. Site: Shoumaa el-Khroub, near
         <a href="/wiki/Constantine,_Algeria" title="Constantine, Algeria">
          Constantine
         </a>
         , Algeria
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 206 BC, there was a quick succession of kings in Eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">
        Numidia
       </a>
       that temporarily ended with the division of the land between Carthage and the Western Numidian king
       <a href="/wiki/Syphax" title="Syphax">
        Syphax
       </a>
       , a former Roman ally. For this bargain, Syphax was to marry
       <a href="/wiki/Sophonisba" title="Sophonisba">
        Sophonisba
       </a>
       , daughter of
       <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Gisco" title="Hasdrubal Gisco">
        Hasdrubal Gisco
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massinissa" title="Massinissa">
        Massinissa
       </a>
       , who had thus lost his
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiancee" title="Fiancee">
        fiancee
       </a>
       , went over to the Romans with whom he had already established contact during his military service in Iberia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011372_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011372-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Central_Mediterranean_.28206.E2.80.93202_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Mediterranean_(206–202_BC)">
        Central Mediterranean (206–202 BC)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Central Mediterranean (206–202 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Carrying_the_war_to_Africa">
        Carrying the war to Africa
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Carrying the war to Africa">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Utica_(203_BC)" title="Battle of Utica (203 BC)">
        Battle of Utica (203 BC)
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Great_Plains" title="Battle of the Great Plains">
        Battle of the Great Plains
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1530" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png/400px-Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png/600px-Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png/800px-Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_204-203_aC.png" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Publius_Cornelius_Scipio" title="Publius Cornelius Scipio">
          Publius Cornelius Scipio
         </a>
         's military campaign in Africa (204–203 B.C.)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 205, Mago landed in
       <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">
        Genua
       </a>
       by sea the remnants of his Spanish army. This was the third Carthaginian force invading Italy. It soon received Gallic and Ligurian reinforcements. Mago's arrival in the north of the Italian peninsula was followed by Hannibal's
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crotona" title="Battle of Crotona">
        Battle of Crotona
       </a>
       in 204 in the south of the peninsula. Mago marched his reinforced army towards the lands of the Boii and Insubres, Carthage's main Gallic allies and a place of retreat for Hasdrubal's defeated remnants. His move was checked by the Romans in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Po_Valley_Raid" title="Po Valley Raid">
        Po Valley Raid
       </a>
       in 203. This hindered the third attempted invasion of Italy early, by preventing Mago from uniting with Hannibal's army in the south. The split Carthaginian armies were less dangerous, allowing for Roman manpower to be directed to the invasion of Africa, despite the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Damocles_sword" title="Damocles sword">
        Damocles sword
       </a>
       of the enemy troops on and around Roman lands.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the same time,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus_Major" title="Scipio Africanus Major">
        Scipio Africanus Major
       </a>
       was given command of the legions in
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       and was allowed to levy volunteers for his plan to end the war by an invasion of Africa. The legions in Sicily were mainly the survivors of Cannae, who were not allowed home until the war was finished. Scipio was also one of the survivors but, unlike the ordinary soldiers, had been allowed to return to Rome along with the other surviving
       <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">
        tribunes
       </a>
       , and had run successfully for public office and had been given command of the troops in Iberia.
      </p>
      <p>
       Within a year of his landing in Africa, Scipio twice routed the regular Carthaginian forces, under
       <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Gisco" title="Hasdrubal Gisco">
        Hasdrubal Gisco
       </a>
       , and his
       <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">
        Numidian
       </a>
       allies. The main native supporter of the Carthaginians, king
       <a href="/wiki/Syphax" title="Syphax">
        Syphax
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Masaesyli" title="Masaesyli">
        Massaesylians
       </a>
       (western Numidians), was defeated and taken prisoner.
       <a href="/wiki/Masinissa" title="Masinissa">
        Masinissa
       </a>
       , a Numidian rival of Syphax and, at that time, an ally of the Romans, seized a large part of his kingdom with their help. These setbacks persuaded some of the Carthaginians that it was time to sue for peace. Others pleaded for the recall of the sons of
       <a href="/wiki/Hamilcar_Barca" title="Hamilcar Barca">
        Hamilcar Barca
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">
        Hannibal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mago_(Barcid)" title="Mago (Barcid)">
        Mago
       </a>
       , who were still fighting the Romans in Bruttium and
       <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">
        Cisalpine Gaul
       </a>
       respectively.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 203 BC, while
       <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">
        Scipio
       </a>
       was carrying all before him in Africa and the Carthaginian peace party were arranging an
       <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">
        armistice
       </a>
       , Hannibal was recalled from Italy by the war party at Carthage. After leaving a record of his expedition engraved in
       <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">
        Punic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       upon bronze tablets in the temple of
       <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">
        Juno
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Crotone" title="Crotone">
        Crotone
       </a>
       , he sailed back to Africa. These records were later quoted by
       <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">
        Polybius
       </a>
       . Hannibal's arrival immediately restored the predominance of the war party, who placed him in command of a combined force of
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        African
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Conscription#Medieval_levies" title="Conscription">
        levies
       </a>
       and his mercenaries from Italy. But Hannibal was opposed to this policy and tried to convince them not to send the untrained African levies into battle. In 202 BC, Hannibal met Scipio in a peace conference. Despite the two generals' mutual admiration, negotiations foundered, according to the Romans due to "Punic faith", meaning bad faith. This Roman expression referred to the alleged breach of protocols which ended the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">
        First Punic War
       </a>
       by the Carthaginian attack on Saguntum, Hannibal's perceived breaches of what the Romans perceived as military etiquette (i.e., Hannibal's numerous ambuscades), as well as the armistice violated by the Carthaginians in the period before Hannibal's return.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Broken_armistice_and_final_peace_treaty">
        Broken armistice and final peace treaty
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Broken armistice and final peace treaty">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">
        Battle of Zama
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2440" data-file-width="3762" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg/220px-Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg/330px-Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg/440px-Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schlacht_bei_Zama_Gem%C3%A4lde_H_P_Motte.jpg" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         Carthaginian war elephants engage Roman infantry at the Battle of Zama (202 BC).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The decisive battle soon followed. Unlike most battles of the Second Punic War, the Romans had superiority in cavalry and the Carthaginians had superiority in infantry. The Roman army was generally better armed and trained than the Carthaginians. Hannibal had refused to lead this army into battle, because he did not expect them to be able to perform. There had been very bitter arguments between him and the oligarchy. His co-general,
       <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Gisco" title="Hasdrubal Gisco">
        Hasdrubal Gisco
       </a>
       , was forced to commit suicide by a violent mob after he spoke in support of Hannibal's view that such troops should not be led into battle. Before the battle, Hannibal gave no speech to his new troops, only to his veterans.
      </p>
      <p>
       Scipio countered an expected Carthaginian elephant charge, which caused some of Hannibal's elephants to turn back into his own ranks, throwing his cavalry into disarray. The Roman cavalry was able to capitalize on this and drive the Carthaginian cavalry from the field. The battle remained closely fought and, at one point, it seemed that Hannibal was on the verge of victory. However, Scipio was able to rally his men, and his cavalry returned from chasing the Carthaginian cavalry and attacked Hannibal's rear. This two-pronged attack caused the Carthaginian formation to disintegrate and collapse. After their defeat,
       <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">
        Hannibal
       </a>
       convinced the Carthaginians to accept peace. Notably, he broke the rules of the assembly by forcibly removing a speaker who supported continued resistance. Afterwards, he was obliged to apologize for his behaviour.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Eastern_Mediterranean_and_Ionian_Sea_.28206.E2.80.93201_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Mediterranean_and_Ionian_Sea_(206–201_BC)">
        Eastern Mediterranean and Ionian Sea (206–201 BC)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Eastern Mediterranean and Ionian Sea (206–201 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">
        First Macedonian War
       </a>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        206 BC – the Aetolians make peace with Macedonia
       </li>
       <li>
        205 BC – Rome lands with 11,000 men and 35 ships in
        <a href="/wiki/Durr%C3%ABs" title="Durrës">
         Durrës
        </a>
        but achieve no military objective
       </li>
       <li>
        205 BC – the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_war" title="First Macedonian war">
         First Macedonian war
        </a>
        ends with the peace
        <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Phoenice" title="Treaty of Phoenice">
         Treaty of Phoenice
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">
        Aftermath
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Aftermath">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1268" data-file-width="2000" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg/200px-Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg/300px-Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg/400px-Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthage-1958-PortsPuniques.jpg" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         Aerial view (1958) of Carthage's
         <a href="/wiki/Cothon" title="Cothon">
          cothon
         </a>
         -type military harbour. The circular naval base was situated on the central peninsula. The entrance to the harbour has silted up.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="592" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg/200px-Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg/300px-Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg/400px-Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquette_Port_militaire_punique_2.jpg" title="Suurenna">
          </a>
         </div>
         Scale model of the circular naval base at Carthage. Note the individual docking bays for warships.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Carthage lost Hispania forever, and Rome firmly established her power there over large areas. Rome imposed a war indemnity of 10,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talent_(weight)" title="Talent (weight)">
        talents
       </a>
       (300
       <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
        tonnes
       </a>
       /660,000 pounds), limited the Carthaginian navy to 10 ships (to ward off pirates), and forbade Carthage from raising an army without Roman permission. The Numidians took the opportunity to capture and plunder Carthaginian territory. Half a century later, when Carthage raised an army to defend itself from these incursions, Rome destroyed her in the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">
        Third Punic War
       </a>
       (149–146 BC). Rome, on the other hand, by her victory, had taken a key step towards what ultimately became her domination of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       world.
      </p>
      <p>
       The end of the war did not meet with a universal welcome in Rome. When the Senate decided upon a peace treaty with Carthage,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quintus_Caecilius_Metellus_(died_175_BC)" title="Quintus Caecilius Metellus (died 175 BC)">
        Quintus Caecilius Metellus
       </a>
       , a former consul, said he did not look upon the termination of the war as a blessing to Rome, since he feared that the Roman people would now sink back again into its former slumbers, from which it had been roused by the presence of Hannibal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Others, most notably
       <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Elder" title="Cato the Elder">
        Cato the Elder
       </a>
       , feared that if Carthage was not completely destroyed it would soon regain its power and pose new threats to Rome; he pressed for harsher peace conditions. Even after the peace, Cato insisted on the destruction of Carthage, ending all his speeches with
       <a href="/wiki/Carthago_delenda_est" title="Carthago delenda est">
        "Carthage must be destroyed"
       </a>
       , even if they had nothing to do with Carthage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Archaeology has discovered that the famous circular military harbour at Carthage, the
       <a href="/wiki/Cothon" title="Cothon">
        Cothon
       </a>
       , received a significant buildup during or after this war. Though shielded from external sight, it could house and quickly deploy about 200
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triremes" title="Triremes">
        triremes
       </a>
       . This appears a surprising development as, after the war, one of the terms of surrender restricted the Carthaginian fleet to only ten triremes. One possible explanation: as has been pointed out for other Phoenician cities,
       <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">
        privateers
       </a>
       with warships played a significant role besides trade, even when the Roman Empire was fully established and officially controlled all coasts. In this case it is not clear whether the treaty included private warships. The only reference to Carthaginian privateers comes from the First Punic War: one such privateer, Hanno the Rhodian, owned a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinquereme" title="Quinquereme">
        quinquereme
       </a>
       (faster than the serial production models that the Romans had copied), manned with about 500 men and then among the heaviest warships in use. Later pirates in Roman waters are all reported with much smaller vessels, which could outrun naval vessels, but operated with lower personnel costs. Thus piracy was probably highly developed in Carthage and the state did not have a monopoly of military forces. Pirates probably played an important role in capturing slaves, one of the most profitable trade-goods, but merchant ships with tradeable goods and a crew were also their targets. No surviving source reports the fate of Carthaginian privateers in the periods between and after the Punic Wars.
      </p>
      <p>
       Hannibal became a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Businessman" title="Businessman">
        businessman
       </a>
       for several years and later enjoyed a leadership role in Carthage. However, the Carthaginian nobility, upset by his policy of democratisation and his struggle against corruption, persuaded the Romans to force him into exile in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
        Asia Minor
       </a>
       , where he again led armies against the Romans and their allies on the battlefield. He eventually committed suicide (ca. 182 BC) to avoid capture.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Carthage_and_Numidia_after_the_war">
        Carthage and Numidia after the war
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Carthage and Numidia after the war">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Constant low-level warfare persisted between Carthage and Numidia, but, by the time of the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">
        Third Punic War
       </a>
       (149–146 BC), Carthage had lost most of her African territories and the Numidians traded independently with the Greeks.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence">
        Intelligence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Intelligence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In this conflict intelligence played an important role on both sides. Hannibal mastered an intelligence service that enabled him to achieve outstanding victories.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Likewise, Scipio Africanus Major's victories depended on information. In 217 BC a Carthaginian resident spy in Rome—probably a Roman citizen—was caught and had his hands cut off as a punishment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livy_3-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Livy-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.gutenberg.org/files/10907/10907-h/10907-h.htm#d33" rel="nofollow">
          22.33.1
         </a>
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Opinions_on_the_war">
        Opinions on the war
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Opinions on the war">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">
        Livy
       </a>
       it was "the most memorable of all wars that were ever waged: the war which the Carthaginians, under the conduct of Hannibal, maintained with the Roman people. For never did any states and nations more efficient in their resources engage in contest; nor had they themselves at any other period so great a degree of power and energy. They brought into action too no arts of war unknown to each other, but those which had been tried in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Punic_war" title="First Punic war">
        first Punic war
       </a>
       ; and so various was the fortune of the conflict, and so doubtful the victory, that they who conquered were more exposed to danger. The hatred with which they fought also was almost greater than their resources".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livy_3-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Livy-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.gutenberg.org/files/10907/10907-h/10907-h.htm#c1" rel="nofollow">
          21.1
         </a>
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
        In popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Punic_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: In popular culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/G._A._Henty" title="G. A. Henty">
         G. A. Henty
        </a>
        's 1887 historical novel
        <i>
         The Young Carthaginian
        </i>
        tells the story of
        <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">
         Hannibal
        </a>
        and the Second Punic War from the perspective of the fictional character Malchus, a cousin of Hannibal.
       </li>
       <li>
        Hannibal's exploits, as well as Archimedes and the Siege of Syracuse, are dramatically reenacted in the classic early Italian silent film
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cabiria" title="Cabiria">
          Cabiria
         </a>
        </i>
        (1914).
       </li>
       <li>
        In the BBC TV Series
        <i>
         On Hannibal's Trail
        </i>
        (2009)
       </li>
      </ul>

          <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Liv.%2026.40.4&amp;lang=original" rel="nofollow">
           XXVI.40
          </a>
          . According to
          <a href="/wiki/F._W._Walbank" title="F. W. Walbank">
           F. W. Walbank
          </a>
          , p. 84, note 2, "Livy accidentally omits Messenia and erroneously describes Pleuratus as king of Thrace."
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-Livy2-48">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Livy2_48-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">
           Livy
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           The History of Rome by Titus Livius: Books Twenty-Seven to Thirty-Six,
          </i>
          trans. Cyrus Edmonds. London: 1850.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011362-49">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoyos2011362_49-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="#CITEREFHoyos2011">
           Hoyos 2011
          </a>
          , p. 362.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-50">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-50">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Valerius_Maximus" title="Valerius Maximus">
           Valerius Maximus
          </a>
          vii. 2. §3.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-51">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-51">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
           Plutarch
          </a>
          ,
          <a class="external text" href="http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Plutarch/Lives/Cato_Major*.html" rel="nofollow">
           Life of Cato
          </a>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-52">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-52">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">

         </span>
